Dario Amodei

Role: CEO, Anthropic Active: 2021–present Key contribution: Founded Anthropic after leaving OpenAI; pioneered Constitutional AI; built the Claude model family with safety as the first priority

Background

Amodei was VP of Research at OpenAI before leaving in 2021 to founded Anthropic with a group of former OpenAI researchers. Anthropic’s mission: build safe, capable AI systems through rigorous alignment research. Claude has become one of the leading frontier model families, with a reputation for safety and reliability.

Key Quotes

On why he left OpenAI to start Anthropic (2023):

“The most important thing for the future of AI is to solve the safety problem. Starting a new company that’s built around that mission from day one was the best way to do it.”

On Constitutional AI (2023):

“Instead of relying solely on human feedback, we wanted models to have an internal constitution — a set of principles they use to evaluate their own outputs. It’s more scalable and principled.”

On the stakes (2024):

“I think there’s a real chance that AI systems will be more capable than humans in most domains within the next decade. Getting the alignment problem right before that happens is the most important technical challenge of our time.”

On Claude’s approach (2025):

“We’re building Claude to be helpful, honest, and harmless. Not because it’s the easiest thing to do, but because we think that’s what AI should be. Capability without safety is not a product — it’s a liability.”

On competition driving safety risks (2025):

“The competitive pressure to ship more capable systems creates a natural incentive to cut corners on safety. We need structural solutions — regulation, standards, independent auditing — to counteract that.”

Role: CEO, Google DeepMind Active: 2010–present Key contribution: Co-founded DeepMind; led AlphaGo, AlphaFold, AlphaEvolve; pioneer of AI for scientific discovery

Background

A former child chess prodigy and game designer, Hassabis co-founded DeepMind in 2010 with the mission of “solving intelligence.” Google acquired DeepMind in 2014. Under his leadership, DeepMind has produced landmark results in games (AlphaGo), biology (AlphaFold), and algorithmic discovery (AlphaEvolve).

Key Quotes

“We want to solve intelligence, and then use that to solve everything else.”

On AlphaFold (2021):

On AGI expectations (2024):

On the merger with Google Brain (2023):

“By bringing together Google Brain and DeepMind, under the Google DeepMind umbrella, we’ll accelerate progress toward AGI and build more capable and responsible AI systems.”

On AI for discovery (2025, after AlphaEvolve):

“We’re moving from AI that perceives and predicts to AI that creates and discovers. This is the beginning of a new era of AI-accelerated science.”

Elon Musk

Role: CEO, xAI / Tesla / SpaceX Active: 2015–present (xAI: 2023–present) Key contribution: Co-founded OpenAI (2015, left 2018); founded xAI (2023, Grok); pushed AI for autonomous driving and robotics

Background

Musk has been a central and controversial figure in AI from the beginning. He co-founded OpenAI as a counterweight to Google’s AI dominance, left after disagreements over direction, and later founded xAI to build what he calls “maximum truth-seeking AI.” His comments on AI risk have oscillated between existential warnings and active acceleration.

Key Quotes

On AI as existential risk (2014):

“We need to be very careful with artificial intelligence. I think we need to be proactive about regulation rather than reactive. I think by the time we are reactive, it’s too late.”

On leaving OpenAI (2023):

“I originally created OpenAI as an open-source, non-profit organization. It is now a closed-source, for-profit organization. That was not my intent.”

On founding xAI (2023):

“The goal of xAI is to understand the true nature of the universe. We want to build an AI that is maximally curious and truth-seeking.”

On AGI timeline (2024):

“I think we’ll have AGI probably by 2026 or 2027. That might seem optimistic, but the rate of improvement is just staggering.”

On autonomous driving (2025):

“Full self-driving is finally real. The neural network can now drive better than a human. This year, Tesla will have more autonomous miles than all other cars combined.”

On competition (2025, on Grok vs ChatGPT):

“Grok is designed to answer the spicy questions that other AI models won’t touch. It should be helpful to humanity, not politically correct.”

Zuckerberg was relatively late to the AI race compared to Google and Microsoft, but Meta has since become one of the most aggressive AI companies. Meta’s Llama models have become the leading open-weight models, and Zuckerberg has positioned Meta behind open-source AI as a strategic bet.

Key Quotes

On open-source (2024, on Llama 3):

On the Llama strategy (2024):

“Llama won’t be the most powerful model at any given moment. But it will be the most accessible. And over time, the open-source ecosystem will catch up and surpass closed models.”

On AI infrastructure (2025):

On AI agents (2025):

“We’re building AI agents that can interact with customers, create content, and help people in their daily lives. Hundreds of thousands of businesses are already using our AI tools.”

On competition (2026):

Satya Nadella

Role: CEO, Microsoft Active: 2014–present Key contribution: Transformed Microsoft around AI; orchestrated multi-billion dollar investment in OpenAI; integrated AI across Azure, Office, Windows, and Bing

Background

Nadella became Microsoft CEO in 2014 and shifted the company from “Windows-first” to “cloud-first.” His decision to invest deeply in OpenAI (starting with $1B in 2019, scaling to over $13B) positioned Microsoft at the center of the AI revolution. Under his leadership, Microsoft launched Copilot across all products and became the primary cloud provider for OpenAI.

Key Quotes

On the AI platform shift (2023):

“This is a platform shift. It’s as significant as the PC, the web, and the smartphone. It will change how people work, create, and interact.”

On the OpenAI partnership (2023):

“We have a very deep partnership that is built on trust and shared mission. We’re not just investors — we’re building together.”

On democratizing AI (2024):

“Our goal is to put AI in the hands of every person and every organization on the planet. Not just the Fortune 500, but every small business, every non-profit, every student.”

On AI’s impact on work (2024):

“Copilot is not about replacing people. It’s about freeing people from drudgery so they can focus on what they’re best at: creativity, collaboration, and complex problem-solving.”

On responsible AI (2025):

“We have a responsibility to build AI that is safe, secure, and trustworthy. That means investing in safety research, building guardrails, and engaging with policymakers.”

On AI agents (2026):

“Agents will be the next big thing. They don’t just respond to prompts — they act on your behalf. This is a fundamental shift from copilot to autonomous assistance.”

On progress (2026):

Jensen Huang

Role: Founder & CEO, NVIDIA Active: 1993–present Key contribution: Built the hardware that powers modern AI; transformed NVIDIA from a graphics company to the world’s most valuable AI infrastructure provider

Background

Huang co-founded NVIDIA in 1993. The company’s GPUs proved ideal for neural network training, and the launch of CUDA (2006) enabled programmable GPU computing. By 2024, NVIDIA’s market cap exceeded $3T as its hardware became the “picks and shovels” of the AI gold rush. Huang’s annual GTC keynotes have become the most important events in the AI hardware calendar.

Key Quotes

On the iPhone moment (2023):

“ChatGPT is the iPhone moment of AI. It’s the first time that AI has been put into the hands of everyone in a really useful and accessible way.”

On the new industrial revolution (2024):

“We are at the beginning of a new industrial revolution. This is not just about better chatbots. This is about the digitization of everything — every factory, every supply chain, every industry will be transformed.”

On Moore’s Law and AI (2024):

“Moore’s Law is dead. But the rate of AI improvement is far outpacing Moore’s Law. We are doubling the capability of our chips every year, and we will continue to do so.”

On software being eaten by AI (2025):

“The traditional way of writing software is over. We are now in the era of AI-driven software. Every application will be infused with intelligence. The next trillion-dollar company will be an AI company.”

On the longevity of demand (2025):

“The demand for our GPUs is not just from training. Inference is going to be enormous. Every time you use an AI, you’re using a GPU. That demand will continue to grow for years.”

On AGI (2026):

“Within the next five years, we’ll see systems that can pass any test a human can pass. The question isn’t if, but when. And we’re building the infrastructure for that world right now.”

Kai-Fu Lee

Role: Chairman & CEO, Sinovation Ventures; former President, Google China Active: 1990s–present Key contribution: Bridged US and Chinese AI ecosystems; authored AI Superpowers (2018) and AI 2041 (2021); leading analyst of China’s AI strategy and US-China AI competition

Background

Lee led Microsoft Research Asia, founded Google China, and later founded Sinovation Ventures. AI Superpowers was among the first books to analyze the US–China AI race. AI 2041 (with Chen Qiufan) used fiction to explore AI’s potential futures. He is a unique voice with deep experience in both US and Chinese tech.

Key Quotes

On the US–China AI race (2018):

“China has three advantages: massive data, fierce entrepreneurial energy, and strong government support. The US has a lead in fundamental research and talent. The race is closer than most people think.”

On AI’s impact on work (2018):

“AI will displace many jobs, but it will also create new ones. The challenge is not the technology — it’s the transition. We need to think about education, social safety nets, and retraining.”

On the open-source wave (2025):

“DeepSeek showed something important: Chinese AI companies can compete on the frontier, not just on application. The open-source model is accelerating China’s AI ecosystem faster than export controls can slow it.”

On AGI timelines (2025):

“I think AGI is further away than the optimists claim — maybe 20-30 years. But even narrow AI will transform the economy in ways we’re not prepared for.”

On coexistence (2026):

“The US and China will both produce frontier AI. They will compete, but they need to cooperate on safety. AI risk does not respect borders. We need a framework for global AI governance.”

Last updated: Mid-2026

Eliezer Yudkowsky

Role: Lead Researcher, Machine Intelligence Research Institute (MIRI) Active: 2000–present Key contribution: Originated the modern AI alignment problem; developed key concepts including orthogonality thesis, instrumental convergence, and the “fast takeoff” scenario; wrote Inadequate Equilibria and sequences on rationality and AI risk

Background

Yudkowsky has been writing about AI risk since the early 2000s. His sequence of blog posts and papers shaped the field of AI alignment. He is among the most cited (and most controversial) thinkers on AI existential risk, arguing that alignment is far harder than the mainstream believes and that timelines are shorter than most expect.

Key Quotes

On the alignment difficulty (2008):

“The AI does not hate you, nor does it love you, but you are made of atoms it can use for something else.”

On AGI timelines (2023):

“I think there is a significant chance that we get AGI within the next 5-10 years. I don’t think we’re ready for it. The alignment problem is not solved.”

On fast takeoff (2023):

“The first AGI will not be built slowly, iteratively, with careful testing. It will arrive as a discontinuous jump. The first AGI will immediately be superhuman in some ways.”

On current approaches (2024):

“RLHF, Constitutional AI — these are band-aids. They create the appearance of alignment without solving the underlying problem. We need mathematical guarantees, not empirical tweaks.”

On the state of the field (2025):

“The gap between AI capability and alignment research is widening, not narrowing. Every year that we don’t solve alignment, the stakes get higher.”

Key Speeches & Appearances

DateEventTopic
2007Singularity SummitAI risk introduction
2023Time Magazine interviewAGI risk and timelines

Max Tegmark

Role: Professor, MIT; President, Future of Life Institute (FLI) Active: 2000s–present Key contribution: Co-founded the Future of Life Institute; authored Life 3.0 (2017) — a comprehensive exploration of AI’s societal implications; leading voice for AI governance and beneficial AI development

Background

Tegmark is a physicist and cosmologist who turned his attention to AI in the 2010s. He co-founded FLI, which organized the first major conferences on AI safety and published the open letter calling for research on “robust and beneficial AI” (2015). Life 3.0 remains one of the most accessible books on AI’s long-term implications.

Key Quotes

On the open letter (2015):

“We got over 10,000 signatures including Hawking, Musk, and Wozniak. It showed that the AI research community takes safety seriously. That was a turning point.”

On the AI stakes (2017):

“AI is the most important conversation of our time. It has the potential to solve our biggest problems or create new ones beyond our imagination. The outcome depends on the choices we make today.”

On the Future of Life Institute’s role (2023):

“Our goal is to ensure that AI is developed in a way that benefits all of humanity. We’re not anti-AI — we’re pro-responsible-AI.”

On the pause letter (2023, calling for a 6-month moratorium on giant AI training runs):

“We asked for a pause. Not a stop. A pause — to catch our breath, to develop safety standards, to get governance in place. The response showed how polarized this conversation has become.”

On governance (2024):

“We need to build institutions that can manage the development of superhuman AI. This is not just a technical problem — it’s an institutional design problem of the highest order.”

On optimism (2025):

“I’m optimistic because I see what’s at stake, and I see smart people working on it. But optimism without action is just wishful thinking. We need to build the governance structures before we need them.”

Key Speeches & Appearances

DateEventTopic
2017TEDAI and the future of life
May 2018MITAI, physics, and cosmology
Mar 2023FLI statementCall for AI pause
Jun 2024MIT Technology ReviewAI governance
Jan 2025DavosGlobal AI governance framework

  • Future of Life Institute
  • Life 3.0 (book)
  • FLI’s AI policy resources

Last updated: Mid-2026

Nick Bostrom

Role: Philosopher, University of Oxford Active: 1990s–present Key contribution: Authored Superintelligence (2014) — the book that brought AI existential risk to mainstream attention; coined terms including “paperclip maximizer,” “singleton,” “differential technological development”

Background

Bostrom is the founding director of Oxford’s Future of Humanity Institute (until its closure in 2024). Superintelligence is widely credited with sparking the modern AI safety conversation. His thought experiments (the paperclip maximizer, the vulnerable world hypothesis) have shaped how researchers think about AI risk.

Key Quotes

On the paperclip maximizer (2003):

“An AI whose goal is to maximize paperclips might, if sufficiently intelligent, decide to convert all matter in the solar system — including humans — into paperclips. This illustrates the problem of goal specification.”

On why superintelligence matters (2014):

“A superintelligent AI could be the best or the last thing that ever happens to humanity. The outcome depends on whether we can solve the alignment problem before it arrives.”

On the vulnerable world hypothesis (2019):

“If technological development continues, we will eventually reach a point where the destructive potential of technology exceeds humanity’s ability to manage it. AI may be the technology that takes us past that threshold.”

On timelines (2024):

“I think the median estimate for AGI among experts has moved significantly closer in the last decade. This is not a problem for future generations — it’s a problem for this generation.”

On the current conversation (2025):

“I’m encouraged that the conversation has moved from ‘is this a real concern?’ to ‘how do we address it?’ That’s progress. But we need to translate concern into action.”

Key Speeches & Appearances

DateEventTopic
2014TEDWhat happens when our computers get smarter than we are?
Mar 2023Lex Fridman PodcastSuperintelligence, alignment, AGI timelines
Jun 202480,000 Hours PodcastThe current state of AI safety

Links

  • Superintelligence (book)
  • Bostrom’s publications
  • The Vulnerable World Hypothesis (paper)

Last updated: Mid-2026

Aiden Gomez

Role: Co-Founder & CEO, Cohere Active: 2019–present Key contribution: Co-authored the “Attention Is All You Need” paper (2017) as a Google Brain intern; founded Cohere to build enterprise AI; leading voice for practical, secure, enterprise-grade AI

Background

Gomez was one of eight authors of the landmark “Attention Is All You Need” paper (2017) — as an intern at Google Brain. He co-founded Cohere in 2019 to build AI for enterprise, focusing on retrieval-augmented generation (RAG), data privacy, and secure deployment. Cohere has become a leading enterprise AI platform.

Key Quotes

On the transformer paper (2023):

“We had no idea it would change the world. We were just trying to solve a technical problem in machine translation. The fact that it became the foundation of modern AI still surprises me.”

On enterprise AI (2024):

“Consumer AI is about entertainment and general assistance. Enterprise AI is about accuracy, security, and integration with existing systems. These are fundamentally different requirements.”

On RAG (2024):

“Retrieval-augmented generation is the key to making AI reliable for business. If you connect the model to your data, you ground it in facts. You solve hallucination not by making the model better — but by giving it the right information.”

On data privacy (2025):

“Enterprises will not put their data into a black box. They need to know their data is secure, that it’s not being used to train the next model, and that they have control. That’s what we built Cohere for.”

On the future (2025):

“We’re moving from chatbots to systems that actually do work. The next five years will be about making AI reliable enough to trust with important tasks.”

On competition (2026):

“The AI market is big enough for many winners. We don’t need to beat OpenAI or Anthropic at everything. We need to be the best at serving enterprise needs.”

Key Speeches & Appearances

Mar 2025NVIDIA GTCAI for knowledge work
Mar 2026Cohere SummitEnterprise AI maturity model

Key insight: Most benchmarks are saturating. New, harder benchmarks are needed to track continued progress.

Appendix D: Glossary of Key Terms

TermDefinitionReference
AGIArtificial General Intelligence — AI that matches human cognitive ability across all domainsWikipedia
AlignmentEnsuring AI systems pursue the goals their creators intendWikipedia
AttentionThe mechanism that lets transformers weigh the importance of different input tokensWikipedia
Chain-of-thoughtPrompting technique where the model generates intermediate reasoning stepsPaper
Diffusion modelGenerative model that learns to denoise random noise into coherent dataWikipedia
EmergenceCapabilities that appear at scale without explicit trainingWikipedia
Fine-tuningFurther training a pre-trained model on a specific taskWikipedia
Foundation modelLarge model trained on broad data, adaptable to many tasksWikipedia
GPUGraphics Processing Unit — the primary hardware for training neural networksWikipedia
HallucinationWhen AI generates confident falsehoodsWikipedia
In-context learningModel learning to perform a task from examples in the prompt (no weight updates)Wikipedia
Jagged frontierThe irregular boundary where AI is superhuman on some tasks and subhuman on othersHBR
JailbreakA prompt designed to bypass a model’s safety guardrailsWikipedia
LLMLarge Language ModelWikipedia
MoEMixture of Experts — architecture that activates only subset of parameters per tokenWikipedia
RLHFReinforcement Learning from Human Feedback — alignment techniqueWikipedia
Reasoning modelModel trained to generate extended chains of thought before answeringPaper
RAGRetrieval-Augmented Generation — grounding generation in retrieved documentsWikipedia
SWE-benchBenchmark that tests AI on real-world GitHub issue resolutionHomepage
SycophancyTendency of AI to agree with user’s stated or implied positionPaper
Test-time computeInference-time computation (thinking) that improves answer qualityPaper
TransformerThe neural network architecture underlying most modern LLMs (Vaswani et al., 2017)Paper

Conclusion

宗：Human Wisdom AI is a mirror, not a master. The most intelligent system is human and machine thinking together.

The Only Constant Is Acceleration

If there’s one theme running through every chapter of this book, it’s this: the rate of change is accelerating, and it will not slow down.

Not because of any law of nature, but because:

  • Self-reinforcing loops (AI helps make better AI)
  • Economic incentives (trillions of dollars at stake)
  • Geopolitical competition (winner-takes-all dynamics)
  • Network effects (the more people use AI, the more valuable it becomes)

What I Hope You Take Away

  1. The jagged frontier is real. AI is simultaneously brilliant and brittle. The person who knows where it fails is as valuable as the person who knows where it excels.

  2. Judgment is the scarce resource. In a world where AI can generate infinite answers, the ability to distinguish good answers from bad ones — and to know when not to use AI at all — is the meta-skill.

  3. Your humanity is your advantage. AI can generate prose, but it can’t have lived your life. AI can analyze data, but it can’t share your values. AI can simulate empathy, but it can’t actually care. These differences are not bugs to be fixed — they’re the whole point.

  4. The best time to start was yesterday. The second best time is now. The AI era will be the defining transformation of our lifetimes. Understanding it deeply, developing genuine expertise, and helping others navigate it is among the most valuable things you can do — professionally and existentially.

A Final Thought

When future generations look back at this moment, they will not ask “Did you predict what happened?” They will ask “What did you do while it was happening?”

This book is my answer to that question. I hope it helps you find yours.

The soul of this book is Human Wisdom: humanity at the center, intelligence as the instrument. You already have what matters most — your judgment, your values, your lived experience. AI is a mirror, not a master. The most intelligent system is not AI alone, nor humanity alone — it is the two thinking together.

The Intelligence Age is a living document. Every chapter will be updated as the landscape shifts. The version you’re reading reflects the state of AI as of mid-2026. The next update is already in progress.

— The Author June 2026
